May be an image of one or more people, people standing, footwear and indoor.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"Tonight, I\u2019m highlighting Nikki Giovanni who has worn many different hats. Giovanni is a world-renowned poet, writer, commentator, activist, and educator. She\u2019s been dubbed the \u201cPrincess of Black Poetry\u201d and Oprah even named her a living legend. Throughout the decades, her outspokenness, in her writing and in lectures is known for opening up many young minds. Her writing has forceful ideas about love, race, politics, gender but a large share of its power has always come from the sense that she is telling the truth as she sees it, to whoever happens to hear. Her focus is on the individual and how one has to make a difference in oneself, and thus, in the lives of others.\nGiovanni was born in Knoxville, Tennessee, and grew up in Lincoln Heights, an all-black suburb of Cincinnati, Ohio. As a child, she attended an Episcopal school and went to Fisk University, where\u00a0she graduated with a history degree and honors. \u00a0\nSoon after graduating from Fisk University, she lost her grandmother. To cope with this loss, she turned to writing, poems which were later included in her collections. A goal of hers was to make her work easily accessible and understood by all ages. Her writing, heavily inspired by African-American activists and artists, also reflects the influences of issues of race, gender, sexuality, and the African-American family. Her influential work made her a significant figure during the Black Power and Black Arts movements.\nShe has also won over 75 awards, including several NAACP Image Awards. She holds the key to several different cities and is an Honorary Member of Delta Sigma Theta sorority. In 2004, Giovanni was nominated for a Grammy and is currently a distinguished Professor at Virginia Tech. Dubbed as the \u201cpoet of the Black Revolution,\u201d she has left a multigenerational legacy and her work continues to be recognized for pioneering Black power. Go check out her poem \u201cVote.\u201d #FMF #aWEARness \n\nDesign by: May be an image of one or more people, people standing, footwear and text that says 'EXIT FIRE PROT. BULK FEED'.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"Tonight, I\u2019m highlighting a leading figure in the Harlem Renaissance, Aaron Douglas AKA \u201cthe father of Black American Art\u201d. \n\nDouglas, who only intended to pass through Harlem on his way to Paris, was convinced to stay in the city and work on his art during the height of the Harlem Renaissance. During his time in Harlem, he contributed powerful award-winning illustrations of African American life and struggles to multiple publications. \n\nDouglas became well known for his mural work and by the mid-1930s his work was featured on monumental buildings throughout the city. Douglas used his work to tell the story of African American history. In a series consisting of four murals, Douglas takes his audience from an African setting to slavery and the Reconstruction Era in the US, through the threats of lynching and segregation, to a final mural depicting the movement of African Americans north towards the Harlem Renaissance and the Great Depression. At this time, Douglas was the president of the\u00a0Harlem Artists Guild, an organization designed to create a network of support and inspiration for young artists in New York City.\n\nIn 1937, Douglas was awarded a travel fellowship to go to the South and visit primarily\u00a0Black Universities, including\u00a0Fisk University\u00a0in Nashville, the\u00a0Tuskegee Institute in Alabama, and\u00a0Dillard University\u00a0in\u00a0New Orleans.\n\nAfter receiving a Master of Arts degree in 1944, Douglas moved to Nashville and founded the Art Department at Fisk University. He used his experiences as an artist in the Harlem Renaissance to inspire students to studying African-American history to then expand on the movements of African-American art. \n\nHis legacy as a pioneer of the African-American art movement continues to inspire artists to this day.\n\n#FMF #aWEARness \n\nDesign by: May be an image of one or more people, footwear and text that says 'PAUL ROBESON n'.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"Paul Robeson\u00a0was the definition of a true renaissance man. He was a political activist, world-renowned singer, actor and athlete. He was an All-America football player who won a dozen varsity letters at Rutgers AND was the school's valedictorian. He was also a trained lawyer who graduated from Columbia University. His talent was undeniable but his outspokenness against racism and inequities almost killed his career.\n\nRobeson was the son of a minister who escaped slavery in North Carolina as a teenager, Robeson built an extraordinary life despite the prejudiced sociopolitical issues around him.\u00a0He raised money for miners, advocated for Western decolonization and lobbied the federal government to integrate MLB and pass broad-sweeping antilynching legislation.\n\nDuring the Harlem Renaissance, he starred in the Broadway productions of\u00a0The Emperor Jones\u00a0and\u00a0All God\u2019s Chillun Got Wings.\u00a0He performed renditions of African American folk songs and spirituals alongside musician Lawrence Brown at Provincetown Playhouse in Manhattan.\u00a0Later, his popularity rose when he starred in a production of\u00a0Show Boat\u00a0at the Theatre Royal, Drury Lane in London.\u00a0He became increasingly well-known for his rendition of the song \u201cOl\u2019 Man River.\u201d\u00a0His most notable film roles were in\u00a0Body and Soul\u00a0and\u00a0Show Boat.\n\nIn music and theatre, Robeson became a controversial figure for using his celebrity to advance human rights causes around the world. His push for social justice clashed with the repressive climate of the 1950s, and he was blacklisted. He stopped performing, his passport was revoked as a result of being deemed a threat to national security and his songs disappeared from the radio for years.\n\nHis travel ban ended in 1958 which allowed him to continue performing in concerts across Europe until he became ill and returned to the U.S. In 1976, he died of cerebral vascular disorder at the age of 77 in Philadelphia. \n\nTo this day, he remains a symbol of pride and consciousness \u270a\ud83c\udffd\n\n#FMF #aWEARness #blackhistorymonth \n\nDesign by shared by Coby on February 06, 2022 tagging @jay2kool, and @a3_craaaftz. May be an image of one or more people and footwear.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"The Cotton Club was\u00a0Harlem's premier nightclub in the 1920s and 1930s during the Prohibition Era. The Cotton Club sat smack in the center of the Harlem Renaissance as it was instrumental in displaying Black talent. What people may not know, is that before the venue became the widely known Cotton Club, it was owned by first Black heavyweight boxing champ Joe Johnson. It was then called Club Deluxe. In 1922, a leading underworld figure (Owen Madden) in Manhattan purchased the venue and the history below describes the changes throughout the establishment.\n\nIn 1922 and beyond, most of the staff and all of the performers were Black but was an all-white audience, Black patrons were not allowed inside. The racist name and imagery of the club depicted Black people as savages in exotic jungles or as Southern plantation slaves.\n\u00a0\nThe club featured many of the greatest African American entertainers of the era like Count Basie, Ella Fitzgerald, Fats Waller, Louis Armstrong, Dizzy Gillespie, Nat King Cole, Billie Holiday, and Ethel Waters to name a few. Dancers at the Cotton Club were held to strict standards, they had to be at least 5\u20196\u201d tall, light skinned with only a slight tan, and under twenty-one years of age.\n\u00a0\nThe oppressive\u00a0segregation\u00a0of the Cotton Club was reinforced by its depiction of the African American employees as exotic savages or plantation residents. The\u00a0music\u00a0was often orchestrated to bring to mind a jungle atmosphere. By transforming the club into this plantation atmosphere and bringing in celebrities, Owen Madden created a demand for the Cotton Club and its exclusionary policies and also helped perpetuate widely held stereotypes about African Americans.\n\n#FMF #aWEARness #blackhistorymonth #bhm \n\nDesign by: This year, I\u2019m focusing on Black creatives and related times in history that aren\u2019t always mentioned. \n\nThe Harlem Renaissance was a golden age for African American artists, writers and musicians. It gave artists pride and control over how the Black experience was represented in American culture and set the stage for the\u00a0Civil Rights movement.\n\u00a0\nMany in the Harlem Renaissance were part of the early 20th century\u00a0Great Migration\u00a0out of the South into neighborhoods in the\u00a0Northeast\u00a0and\u00a0Midwest. Harlem attracted more than 175,000 African Americans \u2013 making it one of the largest concentrations of black people at the time. These people sought a better standard of living and relief from the institutionalized racism in the South. \n\nArtists in Harlem continue to be an important cultural force through the decades. May be an image of 1 person, standing and footwear.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"What does Black History Month mean to you?\n\nBlack History and culture should be celebrated all year round \ud83d\udcaf \n\nPresident Gerald Ford officially recognized Black History Month in 1976, calling the public to \u201cseize the opportunity to honor the too-often neglected accomplishments of Black Americans in every area of endeavor throughout our history.\u201d \n\nBlack History Month is a time to honor the contributions and legacy of African Americans across US history and society\u2014from activists and civil rights pioneers like Frederick Douglass, Sojourner Truth,\u00a0Marcus Garvey,\u00a0Martin Luther King Jr.,\u00a0Malcolm X and Rosa Parks to leaders and Putting political affiliation and beliefs aside, her story of triumph and accomplishments deserve to be honored.\n\u00a0\n\u201cThe essence of America - that which really unites us - is not ethnicity, or nationality or religion - it is an idea - and what an idea it is: That you can come from humble circumstances and do great things.\u201d \ud83d\udcaf \n\u00a0\nOn this exact day, 17 years ago, Rice was appointed Secretary of State, at the time, making her the highest ranking African American woman ever to serve in a presidential cabinet. She served as the 66th United States Secretary of State from 2005 to 2009 and as the 20th National Security Advisor from 2001 to 2005. She now leads Hoover Institution at Stanford University.\n\u00a0\nRice was born in Birmingham, Alabama and grew up while the South was racially segregated. shared by Coby on January 12, 2022 tagging @a3_craaaftz. May be an image of 1 person.","edge_media_to_caption":{"edges":[{"node":{"text":"Higher education began as a privileged institution designed to advance White people and exclude Black and Brown people. Although generations have fought to broaden access to universities, privilege continues to shape higher learning now. I want to highlight a leading activist, lawyer, administrator and educator Ada Lois Sipuel. On January 14, 1946 the OU Law School rejected her application for admission. Sipuel's attorneys filed a lawsuit alleging that the failure of the state to provide a comparable law school for African American students required that she be admitted. Losing in state courts Marshall argued Sipuel v. Board of Regents of the University of Oklahoma before the US Supreme Court. Saying the school must open its doors because it offered no comparable facility and \"separate but equal\" should be abandoned. \n74 years ago to THE DAY the Supreme Court ruled that she was entitled to a legal education and that Oklahoma must provide instruction for Blacks equal to that of Whites. In order to comply, Oklahoma officials stonewalled efforts to admit Sipuel to OU by constructing a makeshift black law school. More litigation was necessary to prove that this law school was inferior to the OU law school. The president of OU law admitted Sipuel making her the FIRST African American woman to attend an all white law school in the South. \nSipuel didn\u2019t have it easy inside the classroom having to sit in a segregated seat marked \"colored\" that was roped off from the rest of the class. She also had to eat in a separate chained-off guarded area in the cafeteria. Her lawsuit and tuition were supported by hundreds of small donations and she believed she owed it to those donors to succeed. She graduated in 1951 with a law degree from OU.  After passing the bar she practiced law until 1956, when she left legal practice to work as the PR director for Langston University. She eventually became head of the school's social science dept. She returned to OU, where she earned a master's degree and an honorary doctorate from the law school. \nSipuel's life came full circle when she was appointed by Governor David Walters as a member of the Board of Regents of OU. #FMF
